				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>

<p>The <b>University of Oxford</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Collegiate_university" title="Collegiate university">collegiate</a> <a href="/wiki/Research_university" title="Research university">research university</a> in <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford, England</a>. There is evidence of teaching as far back as 1096,<sup id="cite_ref-OxHist_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxHist-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> making it the oldest university in the <a href="/wiki/English-speaking_world" title="English-speaking world">English-speaking world</a> and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_oldest_universities_in_continuous_operation" title="List of oldest universities in continuous operation">world's second-oldest university in continuous operation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OxHist_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxHist-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> It grew rapidly from 1167 when <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry II</a> banned English students from attending the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">University of Paris</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OxHist_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxHist-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> After disputes between students and Oxford townsfolk in 1209, some academics fled north-east to <a href="/wiki/Cambridge" title="Cambridge">Cambridge</a> where they established what became the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Early_records_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Early_records-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The two "<a href="/wiki/Ancient_university" title="Ancient university">ancient universities</a>" are frequently jointly referred to as "<a href="/wiki/Oxbridge" title="Oxbridge">Oxbridge</a>". The history and influence of the University of Oxford has made it one of the most prestigious universities in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The university is made up of <a href="/wiki/Colleges_of_the_University_of_Oxford" title="Colleges of the University of Oxford">38 constituent colleges</a>, and a range of academic departments which are organised into four divisions.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> All the colleges are self-governing institutions within the university, each controlling its own membership and with its own internal structure and activities.<sup id="cite_ref-CollegesandHalls_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CollegesandHalls-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> It does not have a main campus, and its buildings and facilities are scattered throughout the city centre. <a href="/wiki/Undergraduate_education" title="Undergraduate education">Undergraduate teaching</a> at Oxford is organised around weekly <a href="/wiki/Tutorial_system" title="Tutorial system">tutorials</a> at the colleges and halls, supported by classes, lectures, seminars, and laboratory work provided by university faculties and departments; some <a href="/wiki/Postgraduate_education" title="Postgraduate education">postgraduate teaching</a> includes tutorials organised by faculties and departments. It operates the world's oldest <a href="/wiki/Ashmolean_Museum" title="Ashmolean Museum">university museum</a>, as well as the largest <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University_Press" title="Oxford University Press">university press</a> in the world<sup id="cite_ref-publishing_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-publishing-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> and the largest academic library system nationwide.<sup id="cite_ref-uls_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uls-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The university is consistently cited as among the world's best.<sup id="cite_ref-Academic_Ranking_of_World_Universities_2018_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Academic_Ranking_of_World_Universities_2018-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-QS_World_University_Rankings_2019_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QS_World_University_Rankings_2019-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Oxford has educated many notable alumni, including <a href="/wiki/List_of_Nobel_laureates_by_university_affiliation#University_of_Oxford" title="List of Nobel laureates by university affiliation">29 Nobel laureates</a>, 27 <a href="/wiki/List_of_Prime_Ministers_of_the_United_Kingdom_by_education" title="List of Prime Ministers of the United Kingdom by education">prime ministers of the United Kingdom</a> and many heads of state and government around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Famous_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Famous-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> As of 2017, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Nobel_laureates_by_university_affiliation#University_of_Oxford" title="List of Nobel laureates by university affiliation">69 Nobel Prize winners</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Fields_Medal_winners_by_university_affiliation" title="List of Fields Medal winners by university affiliation">3 Fields Medalists</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Turing_Award_laureates_by_university_affiliation" title="List of Turing Award laureates by university affiliation">6 Turing Award winners</a> have studied, worked, or held visiting fellowships at the University of Oxford. Its alumni have won 160 <a href="/wiki/Olympic_medal" title="Olympic medal">Olympic medals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-olympics_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-olympics-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Oxford is the home of the <a href="/wiki/Rhodes_Scholarship" title="Rhodes Scholarship">Rhodes Scholarship</a>, one of the world's oldest international scholarships.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a>
<ul>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#Founding"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Founding</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Renaissance_period"><span class="tocnumber">1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Renaissance period</span></a></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Oxford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Oxford" title="Timeline of Oxford">Timeline of Oxford</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Founding">Founding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Oxford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Founding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oxford_-_Balliol_College_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1329613.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Oxford_-_Balliol_College_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1329613.jpg/220px-Oxford_-_Balliol_College_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1329613.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Oxford_-_Balliol_College_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1329613.jpg/330px-Oxford_-_Balliol_College_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1329613.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Oxford_-_Balliol_College_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1329613.jpg/440px-Oxford_-_Balliol_College_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1329613.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="427" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oxford_-_Balliol_College_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1329613.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Balliol_College" class="mw-redirect" title="Balliol College">Balliol College</a> – one of the university's oldest constituent colleges</div></div></div>
<p>The University of Oxford has no known foundation date.<sup id="cite_ref-Preface_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Preface-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Teaching at Oxford existed in some form as early as 1096, but it is unclear when a university came into being.<sup id="cite_ref-OxHist_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxHist-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> It grew quickly in 1167 when English students returned from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">University of Paris</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OxHist_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxHist-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The historian <a href="/wiki/Gerald_of_Wales" title="Gerald of Wales">Gerald of Wales</a> lectured to such scholars in 1188 and the first known foreign scholar, <a href="/wiki/Emo_of_Friesland" title="Emo of Friesland">Emo of Friesland</a>, arrived in 1190. The head of the university had the title of <a href="/wiki/Chancellor" title="Chancellor">chancellor</a> from at least 1201, and the masters were recognised as a <i>universitas</i> or corporation in 1231. The university was granted a royal charter in 1248 during the reign of King <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">Henry III</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After disputes between students and Oxford townsfolk in 1209, some academics fled from the violence to <a href="/wiki/Cambridge" title="Cambridge">Cambridge</a>, later forming the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Early_records_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Early_records-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mob_Quad_from_Chapel_Tower.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Mob_Quad_from_Chapel_Tower.jpg/220px-Mob_Quad_from_Chapel_Tower.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Mob_Quad_from_Chapel_Tower.jpg/330px-Mob_Quad_from_Chapel_Tower.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Mob_Quad_from_Chapel_Tower.jpg/440px-Mob_Quad_from_Chapel_Tower.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mob_Quad_from_Chapel_Tower.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aerial view of <a href="/wiki/Merton_College" class="mw-redirect" title="Merton College">Merton College</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Mob_Quad" title="Mob Quad">Mob Quad</a>, the oldest <a href="/wiki/Quadrangle_(architecture)" title="Quadrangle (architecture)">quadrangle</a> of the university, constructed in the years from 1288 to 1378</div></div></div>
<p>The students associated together on the basis of geographical origins, into two "<a href="/wiki/Nation_(university)" title="Nation (university)">nations</a>", representing the North (<i>northerners</i> or <i>Boreales</i>, who included the <a href="/wiki/English_people" title="English people">English people</a> from north of the <a href="/wiki/River_Trent" title="River Trent">River Trent</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Scots_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Scots people">Scots</a>) and the South (<i>southerners</i> or <i>Australes</i>, who included English people from south of the Trent, the Irish and the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_people" title="Welsh people">Welsh</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-British_History_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-British_History-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> In later centuries, geographical origins continued to influence many students' affiliations when membership of a <a href="/wiki/Colleges_of_the_University_of_Oxford" title="Colleges of the University of Oxford">college</a> or hall became customary in Oxford. In addition, members of many <a href="/wiki/Religious_order" title="Religious order">religious orders</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominicans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franciscan" class="mw-redirect" title="Franciscan">Franciscans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carmelites" title="Carmelites">Carmelites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Augustinians" title="Augustinians">Augustinians</a>, settled in Oxford in the mid-13th century, gained influence and maintained houses or halls for students.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> At about the same time, private benefactors established colleges as self-contained scholarly communities. Among the earliest such founders were <a href="/wiki/William_of_Durham" title="William of Durham">William of Durham</a>, who in 1249 endowed <a href="/wiki/University_College,_Oxford" title="University College, Oxford">University College</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/John_I_de_Balliol" title="John I de Balliol">John Balliol</a>, father of a future <a href="/wiki/John_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="John of Scotland">King of Scots</a>; <a href="/wiki/Balliol_College,_Oxford" title="Balliol College, Oxford">Balliol College</a> bears his name.<sup id="cite_ref-British_History_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-British_History-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Another founder, <a href="/wiki/Walter_de_Merton" title="Walter de Merton">Walter de Merton</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chancellor" title="Lord Chancellor">Lord Chancellor</a> of England and afterwards <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Rochester" title="Bishop of Rochester">Bishop of Rochester</a>, devised a series of regulations for college life;<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Merton_College,_Oxford" title="Merton College, Oxford">Merton College</a> thereby became the model for such establishments at Oxford,<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> as well as at the University of Cambridge. Thereafter, an increasing number of students lived in colleges rather than in halls and religious houses.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1333–34, an attempt by some dissatisfied Oxford scholars to found a new university at <a href="/wiki/Stamford,_Lincolnshire" title="Stamford, Lincolnshire">Stamford, Lincolnshire</a>, was blocked by the universities of Oxford and Cambridge petitioning King <a href="/wiki/Edward_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward III">Edward III</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Thereafter, until the 1820s, no new universities were allowed to be founded in England, even in London; thus, Oxford and Cambridge had a duopoly, which was unusual in large western European countries.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Renaissance_period">Renaissance period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Oxford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Renaissance period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Speed%27s_map_of_Oxford,_1605..jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/John_Speed%27s_map_of_Oxford%2C_1605..jpg/220px-John_Speed%27s_map_of_Oxford%2C_1605..jpg" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/John_Speed%27s_map_of_Oxford%2C_1605..jpg/330px-John_Speed%27s_map_of_Oxford%2C_1605..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/John_Speed%27s_map_of_Oxford%2C_1605..jpg/440px-John_Speed%27s_map_of_Oxford%2C_1605..jpg 2x" data-file-width="2497" data-file-height="2018" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Speed%27s_map_of_Oxford,_1605..jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In 1605 Oxford was still a walled city, but several colleges had been built outside the city walls (north is at the bottom on this map)</div></div></div>
<p>The new learning of the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> greatly influenced Oxford from the late 15th century onwards. Among university scholars of the period were <a href="/wiki/William_Grocyn" title="William Grocyn">William Grocyn</a>, who contributed to the revival of <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek language</a> studies, and <a href="/wiki/John_Colet" title="John Colet">John Colet</a>, the noted <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Theology" class="mw-redirect" title="Biblical Theology">biblical scholar</a>.
</p><p>With the <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">English Reformation</a> and the breaking of communion with the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>, <a href="/wiki/Recusancy" title="Recusancy">recusant</a> scholars from Oxford fled to continental Europe, settling especially at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Douai" title="University of Douai">University of Douai</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The method of teaching at Oxford was transformed from the medieval <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">scholastic method</a> to Renaissance education, although institutions associated with the university suffered losses of land and revenues. As a centre of learning and scholarship, Oxford's reputation declined in the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a>; enrolments fell and teaching was neglected.
</p><p>In 1636<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Laud" title="William Laud">William Laud</a>, the chancellor and <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>, codified the university's statutes. These, to a large extent, remained its governing regulations until the mid-19th century. Laud was also responsible for the granting of a charter securing privileges for the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University_Press" title="Oxford University Press">University Press</a>, and he made significant contributions to the <a href="/wiki/Bodleian_Library" title="Bodleian Library">Bodleian Library</a>, the main library of the university. From the beginnings of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Established_church" class="mw-redirect" title="Established church">established church</a> until 1866, membership of the church was a requirement to receive the BA degree from the university and "<a href="/wiki/Dissenter" title="Dissenter">dissenters</a>" were only permitted to receive the MA in 1871.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ChristChurchOxfordEngraving1742.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/ChristChurchOxfordEngraving1742.jpg/220px-ChristChurchOxfordEngraving1742.jpg" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/ChristChurchOxfordEngraving1742.jpg/330px-ChristChurchOxfordEngraving1742.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/ChristChurchOxfordEngraving1742.jpg/440px-ChristChurchOxfordEngraving1742.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1394" data-file-height="1128" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ChristChurchOxfordEngraving1742.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An engraving of Christ Church, Oxford, 1742</div></div></div>
<p>The university was a centre of the <a href="/wiki/Cavalier" title="Cavalier">Royalist</a> party during the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a> (1642–1649), while the town favoured the opposing <a href="/wiki/Roundhead" title="Roundhead">Parliamentarian</a> cause.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> From the mid-18th century onwards, however, the university took little part in political conflicts.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Wadham_College" class="mw-redirect" title="Wadham College">Wadham College</a>, founded in 1610, was the undergraduate college of <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Wren" title="Christopher Wren">Sir Christopher Wren</a>. Wren was part of a brilliant group of experimental scientists at Oxford in the 1650s, the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Philosophical_Club" title="Oxford Philosophical Club">Oxford Philosophical Club</a>, which included <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Robert Hooke</a>. This group held regular meetings at Wadham under the guidance of the College's Warden, <a href="/wiki/John_Wilkins" title="John Wilkins">John Wilkins</a>, and the group formed the nucleus which went on to found the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_period">Modern period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Oxford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Modern period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Students">Students</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Oxford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Students">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Before reforms in the early 19th century the curriculum at Oxford was notoriously narrow and impractical. <a href="/wiki/Spencer_Walpole" title="Spencer Walpole">Sir Spencer Walpole</a>, a historian of contemporary Britain and a senior government official, had not attended any university. He says, "few medical men, few solicitors, few persons intended for commerce or trade, ever dreamed of passing through a university career." He quotes the Oxford University Commissioners in 1852 stating: "The education imparted at Oxford was not such as to conduce to the advancement in life of many persons, except those intended for the ministry."<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, Walpole argued: 
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Among the many deficiencies attending a university education there was, however, one good thing about it, and that was the education which the undergraduates gave themselves. It was impossible to collect some thousand or twelve hundred of the best young man in England, to give them the opportunity of making acquaintance with one another, and full liberty to live their lives in their own way, without evolving in the best among them, some admirable qualities of loyalty, independence, and self-control. If the average undergraduate carried from University little or no learning, which was of any service to him, he carried from it a knowledge of men and respect for his fellows and himself, a reverence for the past, a code of honour for the present, which could not but be serviceable. He had enjoyed opportunities... of intercourse with men, some of whom were certain to rise to the highest places in the Senate, in the Church, or at the Bar. He might have mixed with them in his sports, in his studies, and perhaps in his debating society; and any associations which he had this formed had been useful to him at the time, and might be a source of satisfaction to him in after life.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Out of the students who matriculated in 1840, 65% were sons of professionals (34% were Anglican ministers). After graduation 87% became professionals (59% as Anglican clergy). Out of the students who matriculated in 1870, 59% were sons of professionals (25% were Anglican ministers). After graduation 87% became professionals (42% as Anglican clergy).<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>M.&#160;C. Curthoys and H.&#160;S. Jones argue that the rise of organised sport was one of the most remarkable and distinctive features of the history of the universities of Oxford and Cambridge in the late 19th and early 20th centuries. It was carried over from the athleticism prevalent at the public schools such as <a href="/wiki/Eton_College" title="Eton College">Eton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Winchester_College" title="Winchester College">Winchester</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shrewsbury_School" title="Shrewsbury School">Shrewsbury</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Harrow_School" title="Harrow School">Harrow</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the start of 1914 the university housed about 3,000 undergraduates and about 100 postgraduate students. During the First World War many undergraduates and Fellows joined the armed forces. By 1918 virtually all Fellows were in uniform, and the student population in residence was reduced to 12 per cent<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Cleanup" title="Wikipedia:Cleanup"><span title="Wikipedia:Cleanup">of what?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="//archive.org/details/oxforduniversity00univuoft" class="extiw" title="iarchive:oxforduniversity00univuoft">University Roll of Service</a> records that, in total, 14,792 members of the university served in the war, with 2,716 (18.36%) killed.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Not all the members of the university who served in the Great War were on the Allied side; there is a remarkable memorial to members of New College who served in the German armed forces, bearing the inscription, 'In memory of the men of this college who coming from a foreign land entered into the inheritance of this place and returning fought and died for their country in the war 1914–1918'. During the war years the university buildings became hospitals, cadet schools and military training camps.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reforms">Reforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Oxford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Reforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Two parliamentary commissions in 1852 issued recommendations for Oxford and Cambridge. <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Campbell_Tait" title="Archibald Campbell Tait">Archibald Campbell Tait</a>, former headmaster of Rugby School, was a key member of the Oxford Commission; he wanted Oxford to follow the German and Scottish model in which the professorship was paramount. The Commission's report envisioned a centralised university run predominantly by professors and faculties, with a much stronger emphasis on research. The professional staff should be strengthened and better paid. For students, restrictions on entry should be dropped, and more opportunity given to poorer families. It called for an enlargement of the curriculum, with honours to be awarded in many new fields. Undergraduate scholarships should be open to all Britons. Graduate fellowships should be opened up to all members of the university. It recommended that fellows be released from an obligation for ordination. Students were to be allowed to save money by boarding in the city, instead of in a college.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The system of separate <a href="/wiki/Honour_Moderations" title="Honour Moderations">honour schools</a> for different subjects began in 1802, with Mathematics and <a href="/wiki/Literae_Humaniores" class="mw-redirect" title="Literae Humaniores">Literae Humaniores</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-boase_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boase-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Schools of "Natural Sciences" and "Law, and Modern History" were added in 1853.<sup id="cite_ref-boase_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boase-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> By 1872, the last of these had split into "Jurisprudence" and "Modern History". Theology became the sixth honour school.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In addition to these B.A. Honours degrees, the postgraduate <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Civil_Law" title="Bachelor of Civil Law">Bachelor of Civil Law (B.C.L.)</a> was, and still is, offered.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oxford_back_street_-_geograph.org.uk_-_774471.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Oxford_back_street_-_geograph.org.uk_-_774471.jpg/170px-Oxford_back_street_-_geograph.org.uk_-_774471.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Oxford_back_street_-_geograph.org.uk_-_774471.jpg/255px-Oxford_back_street_-_geograph.org.uk_-_774471.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Oxford_back_street_-_geograph.org.uk_-_774471.jpg/340px-Oxford_back_street_-_geograph.org.uk_-_774471.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oxford_back_street_-_geograph.org.uk_-_774471.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Brasenose_Lane" title="Brasenose Lane">Brasenose Lane</a> in the city centre, a street onto which three colleges back – <a href="/wiki/Brasenose_College" class="mw-redirect" title="Brasenose College">Brasenose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_College,_Oxford" title="Lincoln College, Oxford">Lincoln</a> and <a href="/wiki/Exeter_College,_Oxford" title="Exeter College, Oxford">Exeter</a></div></div></div>
<p>The mid-19th century saw the impact of the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Movement" title="Oxford Movement">Oxford Movement</a> (1833–1845), led among others by the future Cardinal <a href="/wiki/John_Henry_Newman" title="John Henry Newman">John Henry Newman</a>. The influence of the reformed model of German universities reached Oxford via key scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Edward_Bouverie_Pusey" title="Edward Bouverie Pusey">Edward Bouverie Pusey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Jowett" title="Benjamin Jowett">Benjamin Jowett</a> and <a href="/wiki/Max_M%C3%BCller" title="Max Müller">Max Müller</a>.
</p><p>Administrative reforms during the 19th century included the replacement of oral examinations with written entrance tests, greater tolerance for <a href="/wiki/English_Dissenters" title="English Dissenters">religious dissent</a>, and the establishment of four women's colleges. Privy Council decisions in the 20th century (e.g. the abolition of compulsory daily worship, dissociation of the Regius Professorship of Hebrew from clerical status, diversion of colleges' theological bequests to other purposes) loosened the link with traditional belief and practice. Furthermore, although the university's emphasis had historically been on classical knowledge, its curriculum expanded during the 19th century to include scientific and medical studies. Knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> was required for admission until 1920, and Latin until 1960.
</p><p>The University of Oxford began to award doctorates in the first third of the 20th century. The first Oxford DPhil in mathematics was awarded in 1921.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The mid-20th century saw many distinguished continental scholars, displaced by <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazism</a> and communism, relocating to Oxford.
</p><p>The list of distinguished scholars at the University of Oxford is long and includes many who have made major contributions to politics, the sciences, medicine, and literature. More than 50 Nobel laureates and more than 50 world leaders have been affiliated with the University of Oxford.<sup id="cite_ref-Famous_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Famous-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Women.27s_education"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Women's_education">Women's education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Oxford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Women&#039;s education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent">First two women's colleges</div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:LMH_Talbot_Hall.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/LMH_Talbot_Hall.png/200px-LMH_Talbot_Hall.png" width="200" height="166" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/LMH_Talbot_Hall.png/300px-LMH_Talbot_Hall.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/LMH_Talbot_Hall.png/400px-LMH_Talbot_Hall.png 2x" data-file-width="555" data-file-height="461" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Lady_Margaret_Hall,_Oxford" title="Lady Margaret Hall, Oxford">Lady Margaret Hall</a> (1878)</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Somerville_College.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Somerville_College.jpg/200px-Somerville_College.jpg" width="200" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Somerville_College.jpg/300px-Somerville_College.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Somerville_College.jpg/400px-Somerville_College.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Somerville_College,_Oxford" title="Somerville College, Oxford">Somerville College</a> (1879)</div></div></div></div>
<p>The university passed a statute in 1875 allowing examinations for women at roughly undergraduate level;<sup id="cite_ref-Lannon_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lannon-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> for a brief period in the early 1900s, this allowed the "<a href="/wiki/Steamboat_ladies" title="Steamboat ladies">steamboat ladies</a>" to receive <i><a href="/wiki/Ad_eundem_gradum" class="mw-redirect" title="Ad eundem gradum">ad eundem</a></i> degrees from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Dublin" title="University of Dublin">University of Dublin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In June 1878, the <i>Association for the Higher Education of Women</i> (AEW) was formed, aiming for the eventual creation of a college for women in Oxford. Some of the more prominent members of the association were <a href="/wiki/George_Granville_Bradley" title="George Granville Bradley">George Granville Bradley</a>, <a href="/wiki/T._H._Green" class="mw-redirect" title="T. H. Green">T. H. Green</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edward_Stuart_Talbot" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward Stuart Talbot">Edward Stuart Talbot</a>. Talbot insisted on a specifically <a href="/wiki/Anglican" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican">Anglican</a> institution, which was unacceptable to most of the other members. The two parties eventually split, and Talbot's group founded <a href="/wiki/Lady_Margaret_Hall,_Oxford" title="Lady Margaret Hall, Oxford">Lady Margaret Hall</a> in 1878, while T. H. Green founded the non-denominational <a href="/wiki/Somerville_College,_Oxford" title="Somerville College, Oxford">Somerville College</a> in 1879.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Lady Margaret Hall and Somerville opened their doors to their first 21 students (12 from Somerville, 9 from Lady Margaret Hall) in 1879, who attended lectures in rooms above an Oxford baker's shop.<sup id="cite_ref-Lannon_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lannon-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>These first two colleges for women were followed by <a href="/wiki/St_Hugh%27s_College,_Oxford" title="St Hugh&#39;s College, Oxford">St Hugh's</a> (1886),<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/St_Hilda%27s_College,_Oxford" title="St Hilda&#39;s College, Oxford">St Hilda's</a> (1893)<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/St_Anne%27s_College,_Oxford" title="St Anne&#39;s College, Oxford">St Anne's College</a> (1952).<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> All of these colleges later became coeducational, starting with <a href="/wiki/Lady_Margaret_Hall,_Oxford" title="Lady Margaret Hall, Oxford">Lady Margaret Hall</a> and <a href="/wiki/St_Anne%27s_College,_Oxford" title="St Anne&#39;s College, Oxford">St Anne's</a> in 1979,<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> and finishing with <a href="/wiki/St_Hilda%27s_College,_Oxford" title="St Hilda&#39;s College, Oxford">St Hilda's</a>, which began to accept male students in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> In the early 20th century, Oxford and Cambridge were widely perceived to be bastions of <a href="/wiki/Male_privilege" title="Male privilege">male privilege</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> however the integration of women into Oxford moved forward during the First World War. In 1916 women were admitted as medical students on a par with men, and in 1917 the university accepted financial responsibility for women's examinations.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 7 October 1920 women became eligible for admission as full members of the university and were given the right to take degrees.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> In 1927 the university's dons created a quota that limited the number of female students to a quarter that of men, a ruling which was not abolished until 1957.<sup id="cite_ref-Lannon_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lannon-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> However, during this period Oxford colleges were <a href="/wiki/Single-sex_education" title="Single-sex education">single sex</a>, so the number of women was also limited by the capacity of the women's colleges to admit students. It was not until 1959 that the women's colleges were given full collegiate status. 
</p><p>In 1974, <a href="/wiki/Brasenose_College,_Oxford" title="Brasenose College, Oxford">Brasenose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jesus_College,_Oxford" title="Jesus College, Oxford">Jesus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wadham_College,_Oxford" title="Wadham College, Oxford">Wadham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hertford_College,_Oxford" title="Hertford College, Oxford">Hertford</a> and <a href="/wiki/St_Catherine%27s_College,_Oxford" title="St Catherine&#39;s College, Oxford">St Catherine's</a> became the first previously all-male colleges to admit women.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Women_at_Oxford_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Women_at_Oxford-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The majority of men's colleges accepted their first female students in 1979,<sup id="cite_ref-Women_at_Oxford_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Women_at_Oxford-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> with <a href="/wiki/Christ_Church,_Oxford" title="Christ Church, Oxford">Christ Church</a> following in 1980,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (May 2018)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and <a href="/wiki/Oriel_College,_Oxford" title="Oriel College, Oxford">Oriel</a> becoming the last men's college to admit women in 1985.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Most of Oxford's graduate colleges were founded as coeducational establishments in the 20th century, with the exception of St Anthony's, which was founded as a men's college in 1950 and began to accept women only in 1962.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> By 1988, 40% of undergraduates at Oxford were female;<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> in 2016, 45% of the student population, and 47% of undergraduate students, were female.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In June 2017, Oxford announced that starting the following academic year, history students may choose to sit a take-home exam in some courses, with the intention that this will equalise rates of firsts awarded to women and men at Oxford.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> That same summer, maths and computer science tests were extended by 15 minutes, in a bid to see if female student scores would improve.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The detective novel <i><a href="/wiki/Gaudy_Night" title="Gaudy Night">Gaudy Night</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_L._Sayers" title="Dorothy L. Sayers">Dorothy L. Sayers</a>, herself one of the first women to gain an academic degree from Oxford, is largely set in the all-female <a href="/wiki/List_of_fictional_Oxford_colleges" title="List of fictional Oxford colleges">Shrewsbury College, Oxford</a> (based on Sayers' own <a href="/wiki/Somerville_College,_Oxford" title="Somerville College, Oxford">Somerville College</a><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>), and the issue of women's education is central to its plot. Social historian and Somerville College alumna <a href="/wiki/Jane_Robinson_(historian)" title="Jane Robinson (historian)">Jane Robinson</a>'s book <i>Bluestockings: A Remarkable History of the First Women to Fight for an Education</i> gives a very detailed and immersive account of this history.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Buildings_and_sites">Buildings and sites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Oxford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Buildings and sites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Map">Map</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Oxford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Map">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Main_sites">Main sites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Oxford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Main sites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chemistry_Research_Laboratory_Atrium.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Chemistry_Research_Laboratory_Atrium.JPG/220px-Chemistry_Research_Laboratory_Atrium.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Chemistry_Research_Laboratory_Atrium.JPG/330px-Chemistry_Research_Laboratory_Atrium.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Chemistry_Research_Laboratory_Atrium.JPG/440px-Chemistry_Research_Laboratory_Atrium.JPG 2x" data-file-width="896" data-file-height="672" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chemistry_Research_Laboratory_Atrium.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Atrium of the Chemistry Research Laboratory, where the university has invested heavily in new facilities in recent years</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sheldonian_Theatre_Oxford.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Sheldonian_Theatre_Oxford.jpg/220px-Sheldonian_Theatre_Oxford.jpg" width="220" height="245" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Sheldonian_Theatre_Oxford.jpg/330px-Sheldonian_Theatre_Oxford.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Sheldonian_Theatre_Oxford.jpg/440px-Sheldonian_Theatre_Oxford.jpg 2x" data-file-width="575" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sheldonian_Theatre_Oxford.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Sheldonian_Theatre" title="Sheldonian Theatre">Sheldonian Theatre</a>, built by <a href="/wiki/Sir_Christopher_Wren" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Christopher Wren">Sir Christopher Wren</a> between 1664 and 1668, hosts the university's <a href="/wiki/Congregation_(university)" title="Congregation (university)">Congregation</a>, as well as concerts and degree ceremonies</div></div></div>
<p>The university is a "city university" in that it does not have a main campus; instead, colleges, departments, accommodation, and other facilities are scattered throughout the city centre. The <a href="/wiki/Science_Area,_Oxford" title="Science Area, Oxford">Science Area</a>, in which most science departments are located, is the area that bears closest resemblance to a campus. The ten-acre (4 hectare) <a href="/wiki/Radcliffe_Observatory_Quarter" title="Radcliffe Observatory Quarter">Radcliffe Observatory Quarter</a> in the northwest of the city is currently under development. However, the larger colleges' sites are of similar size to these areas.
</p><p>Iconic university buildings include the <a href="/wiki/Radcliffe_Camera" title="Radcliffe Camera">Radcliffe Camera</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sheldonian_Theatre" title="Sheldonian Theatre">Sheldonian Theatre</a> used for music concerts, lectures, and university ceremonies, and the <a href="/wiki/Examination_Schools" title="Examination Schools">Examination Schools</a>, where examinations and some lectures take place. The <a href="/wiki/University_Church_of_St_Mary_the_Virgin" title="University Church of St Mary the Virgin">University Church of St Mary the Virgin</a> was used for university ceremonies before the construction of the Sheldonian. <a href="/wiki/Christ_Church_Cathedral,_Oxford" title="Christ Church Cathedral, Oxford">Christ Church Cathedral</a> uniquely serves as both a college chapel and as a cathedral.
</p><p>In 2012–13, the university built the controversial one-hectare (400m × 25m) <a href="/wiki/Castle_Mill" title="Castle Mill">Castle Mill</a> development of 4–5-storey blocks of student flats overlooking <a href="/wiki/Cripley_Meadow" title="Cripley Meadow">Cripley Meadow</a> and the historic <a href="/wiki/Port_Meadow" class="mw-redirect" title="Port Meadow">Port Meadow</a>, blocking views of the spires in the city centre.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The development has been likened to building a "skyscraper beside <a href="/wiki/Stonehenge" title="Stonehenge">Stonehenge</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parks">Parks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Oxford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Parks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oxford_Botanic_Garden_in_Autumn_2004.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Oxford_Botanic_Garden_in_Autumn_2004.jpg/220px-Oxford_Botanic_Garden_in_Autumn_2004.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Oxford_Botanic_Garden_in_Autumn_2004.jpg/330px-Oxford_Botanic_Garden_in_Autumn_2004.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Oxford_Botanic_Garden_in_Autumn_2004.jpg/440px-Oxford_Botanic_Garden_in_Autumn_2004.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1523" data-file-height="999" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oxford_Botanic_Garden_in_Autumn_2004.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Autumn in the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Botanic_Garden" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxford Botanic Garden">Botanic Garden</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/University_Parks" title="University Parks">University Parks</a> are a 70-acre (28 ha) parkland area in the northeast of the city, near <a href="/wiki/Keble_College,_Oxford" title="Keble College, Oxford">Keble College</a>, <a href="/wiki/Somerville_College,_Oxford" title="Somerville College, Oxford">Somerville College</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lady_Margaret_Hall,_Oxford" title="Lady Margaret Hall, Oxford">Lady Margaret Hall</a>. It is open to the public during daylight hours. As well as providing gardens and exotic plants, the Parks contains numerous sports fields, used for official and unofficial fixtures, and also contains sites of special interest including the Genetic Garden, an experimental garden to elucidate and investigate evolutionary processes.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford_Botanic_Garden" title="University of Oxford Botanic Garden">Botanic Garden</a> on the <a href="/wiki/High_Street,_Oxford" title="High Street, Oxford">High Street</a> is the oldest <a href="/wiki/Botanical_garden" title="Botanical garden">botanic garden</a> in the UK. It contains over 8,000 different plant species on 1.8&#160;ha (<span class="frac nowrap">4<span class="visualhide">&#160;</span><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span> acres). It is one of the most diverse yet compact major collections of plants in the world and includes representatives of over 90% of the higher plant families. The <a href="/wiki/Harcourt_Arboretum" title="Harcourt Arboretum">Harcourt Arboretum</a> is a 130-acre (53&#160;ha) site six miles (10&#160;km) south of the city that includes native woodland and 67 acres (27 hectares) of meadow. The 1,000-acre (4.0&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) <a href="/wiki/Wytham_Woods" class="mw-redirect" title="Wytham Woods">Wytham Woods</a> are owned by the university and used for research in <a href="/wiki/Zoology" title="Zoology">zoology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>.
</p><p>There are also various collegiate-owned open spaces open to the public, including <a href="/wiki/Bagley_Wood" title="Bagley Wood">Bagley Wood</a> and most notably <a href="/wiki/Christ_Church_Meadow,_Oxford" title="Christ Church Meadow, Oxford">Christ Church Meadow</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organisation">Organisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Oxford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Organisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Departments_of_the_University_of_Oxford" title="Category:Departments of the University of Oxford">Category:Departments of the University of Oxford</a></div>
<p>As a <a href="/wiki/Collegiate_university" title="Collegiate university">collegiate university</a>, Oxford's structure can be confusing to those unfamiliar with it. The university is a federation, comprising over forty self-governing <a href="/wiki/Colleges_of_the_University_of_Oxford" title="Colleges of the University of Oxford">colleges</a> and <a href="/wiki/Permanent_Private_Hall" title="Permanent Private Hall">halls</a>, along with a central administration headed by the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Vice-Chancellors_of_the_University_of_Oxford" title="List of Vice-Chancellors of the University of Oxford">Vice-Chancellor</a>.
</p><p>Academic departments are located centrally within the structure of the federation; they are not affiliated with any particular college. Departments provide facilities for teaching and research, determine the syllabi and guidelines for the teaching of students, perform research, and deliver lectures and seminars.
</p><p>Colleges arrange the tutorial teaching for their undergraduates, and the members of an academic department are spread around many colleges. Though certain colleges do have subject alignments (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Nuffield_College" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuffield College">Nuffield College</a> as a centre for the social sciences), these are exceptions, and most colleges will have a broad mix of academics and students from a diverse range of subjects. Facilities such as libraries are provided on all these levels: by the central university (the <a href="/wiki/Bodleian" class="mw-redirect" title="Bodleian">Bodleian</a>), by the departments (individual departmental libraries, such as the English Faculty Library), and by colleges (each of which maintains a multi-discipline library for the use of its members).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_governance">Central governance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Oxford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Central governance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The university's formal head is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Chancellors_of_the_University_of_Oxford" title="List of Chancellors of the University of Oxford">Chancellor</a><span id="Chancellor_of_the_University_of_Oxford"></span>, currently <a href="/wiki/Chris_Patten" title="Chris Patten">Lord Patten of Barnes</a>, though as at most British universities, the Chancellor is a titular figure and is not involved with the day-to-day running of the university. The Chancellor is elected by the members of <a href="/wiki/Convocation" title="Convocation">Convocation</a>, a body comprising all graduates of the university, and holds office until death.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wellington_Square_Oxford_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wellington_Square_Oxford_1.jpg/220px-Wellington_Square_Oxford_1.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wellington_Square_Oxford_1.jpg/330px-Wellington_Square_Oxford_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wellington_Square_Oxford_1.jpg/440px-Wellington_Square_Oxford_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wellington_Square_Oxford_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Wellington_Square,_Oxford" title="Wellington Square, Oxford">Wellington Square</a>, the name of which has become synonymous with the university's central administration</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/List_of_Vice-Chancellors_of_the_University_of_Oxford" title="List of Vice-Chancellors of the University of Oxford">Vice-Chancellor</a>, currently <a href="/wiki/Louise_Richardson" title="Louise Richardson">Louise Richardson</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-VCapproval_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VCapproval-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NewVCLouiseRichardson_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewVCLouiseRichardson-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> is the <i>de facto</i> head of the university. Five pro-vice-chancellors have specific responsibilities for education; research; planning and resources; development and external affairs; and personnel and equal opportunities. The University Council is the executive policy-forming body, which consists of the vice-chancellor as well as heads of departments and other members elected by <a href="/wiki/Congregation_(university)" title="Congregation (university)">Congregation</a>, in addition to observers from the <a href="/wiki/OUSU" class="mw-redirect" title="OUSU">students' union</a>. Congregation, the "parliament of the dons", comprises over 3,700 members of the university's academic and administrative staff, and has ultimate responsibility for legislative matters: it discusses and pronounces on policies proposed by the University Council.
</p><p>Two university <a href="/wiki/Proctor_of_the_University_of_Oxford" class="mw-redirect" title="Proctor of the University of Oxford">proctors</a>, elected annually on a rotating basis from two of the colleges, are the internal ombudsmen who make sure that the university and its members adhere to its statutes. This role incorporates student welfare and discipline, as well as oversight of the university's proceedings. The university's professors are collectively referred to as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_professorships_at_the_University_of_Oxford" title="List of professorships at the University of Oxford">Statutory Professors of the University of Oxford</a>. They are particularly influential in the running of the university's graduate programmes. Examples of statutory professors are the <a href="/wiki/Chichele_Professorship" title="Chichele Professorship">Chichele Professorships</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Drummond_Professor_of_Political_Economy" title="Drummond Professor of Political Economy">Drummond Professor of Political Economy</a>. The various academic faculties, departments, and institutes are organised into four <a href="/wiki/Divisions_of_the_University_of_Oxford" title="Divisions of the University of Oxford">divisions</a>, each with its own head and elected board. They are the Humanities division; the Social Sciences Division; the Mathematical, Physical and Life Sciences Division; and the Medical Sciences Division.
</p><p>The University of Oxford is a "public university" in the sense that it receives some public money from the government, but it is a "private university" in the sense that it is entirely self-governing and, in theory, could choose to become entirely private by rejecting public funds.<sup id="cite_ref-OXCHEPS39_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OXCHEPS39-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colleges">Colleges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Oxford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Colleges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Colleges_of_the_University_of_Oxford" title="Colleges of the University of Oxford">Colleges of the University of Oxford</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tom_Quad,_Christ_Church_2004-01-21.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Tom_Quad%2C_Christ_Church_2004-01-21.jpg/220px-Tom_Quad%2C_Christ_Church_2004-01-21.jpg" width="220" height="122" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Tom_Quad%2C_Christ_Church_2004-01-21.jpg/330px-Tom_Quad%2C_Christ_Church_2004-01-21.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Tom_Quad%2C_Christ_Church_2004-01-21.jpg/440px-Tom_Quad%2C_Christ_Church_2004-01-21.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1819" data-file-height="1011" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tom_Quad,_Christ_Church_2004-01-21.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tom_Quad" title="Tom Quad">Tom Quad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christ_Church,_Oxford" title="Christ Church, Oxford">Christ Church</a> in the snow</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Somerville_College_Darbishire_quad_2.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Somerville_College_Darbishire_quad_2.png/220px-Somerville_College_Darbishire_quad_2.png" width="220" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Somerville_College_Darbishire_quad_2.png/330px-Somerville_College_Darbishire_quad_2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Somerville_College_Darbishire_quad_2.png/440px-Somerville_College_Darbishire_quad_2.png 2x" data-file-width="832" data-file-height="693" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Somerville_College_Darbishire_quad_2.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Darbishire quad, <a href="/wiki/Somerville_College,_Oxford" title="Somerville College, Oxford">Somerville College</a>, one of the constituent colleges of the University of Oxford</div></div></div>
<p>To be a member of the university, all students, and most academic staff, must also be a member of a college or hall. There are 38 <a href="/wiki/Colleges_of_the_University_of_Oxford" title="Colleges of the University of Oxford">colleges of the University of Oxford</a> and six <a href="/wiki/Permanent_Private_Hall" title="Permanent Private Hall">Permanent Private Halls</a>, each controlling its membership and with its own internal structure and activities.<sup id="cite_ref-CollegesandHalls_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CollegesandHalls-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Not all colleges offer all courses, but they generally cover a broad range of subjects.
</p><p>The colleges are:
</p>
<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 12em; -webkit-column-width: 12em; column-width: 12em;"><div class="plainlist">

<li><a href="/wiki/File:Balliol_College_Oxford_Coat_Of_Arms.svg" class="image"><img alt="Balliol College Oxford Coat Of Arms.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Balliol_College_Oxford_Coat_Of_Arms.svg/12px-Balliol_College_Oxford_Coat_Of_Arms.svg.png" width="12" height="15" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Balliol_College_Oxford_Coat_Of_Arms.svg/18px-Balliol_College_Oxford_Coat_Of_Arms.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Balliol_College_Oxford_Coat_Of_Arms.svg/24px-Balliol_College_Oxford_Coat_Of_Arms.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="524" data-file-height="659" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Balliol_College,_Oxford" title="Balliol College, Oxford">Balliol</a></li>

<p>The Permanent Private Halls were founded by different Christian denominations. One difference between a college and a PPH is that whereas colleges are governed by the fellows of the college, the governance of a PPH resides, at least in part, with the corresponding Christian denomination. The six current PPHs are:
</p>
<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 12em; -webkit-column-width: 12em; column-width: 12em;"><div class="plainlist">

</div></div>
<p>The PPHs and colleges join together as the Conference of Colleges, which represents the common concerns of the several <a href="/wiki/Colleges_of_the_University_of_Oxford" title="Colleges of the University of Oxford">colleges</a> of the university, to discuss matters of shared interest and to act collectively when necessary, such as in dealings with the central university.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The Conference of Colleges was established as a recommendation of the <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Franks,_Baron_Franks" title="Oliver Franks, Baron Franks">Franks</a> Commission in 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Teaching members of the colleges (i.e. fellows and tutors) are collectively and familiarly known as <a href="/wiki/University_dons" class="mw-redirect" title="University dons">dons</a>, although the term is rarely used by the university itself. In addition to residential and dining facilities, the colleges provide social, cultural, and recreational activities for their members. Colleges have responsibility for admitting undergraduates and organising their tuition; for graduates, this responsibility falls upon the departments. There is no common title for the heads of colleges: the titles used include Warden, Provost, Principal, President, Rector, Master and Dean.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Finances">Finances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Oxford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Finances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1_christ_church_hall_2012.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/1_christ_church_hall_2012.jpg/220px-1_christ_church_hall_2012.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/1_christ_church_hall_2012.jpg/330px-1_christ_church_hall_2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/1_christ_church_hall_2012.jpg/440px-1_christ_church_hall_2012.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4256" data-file-height="2832" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1_christ_church_hall_2012.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dining hall at Christ Church. The hall is an important feature of the typical Oxford college, providing a place to both dine and socialise</div></div></div>
<p>In 2014/15, the university had an income of £1,429m; key sources were research grants (£522.9m) and academic fees (£258.3m).<sup id="cite_ref-financial-statements_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-financial-statements-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The colleges had a total income of £415m,<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While the university has a larger annual income and operating budget, the colleges have a larger aggregate endowment: over £3.8bn compared to the university's £834m.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> The Central University's endowment, along with some of the colleges', is managed by the university's wholly owned endowment management office, Oxford University Endowment Management, formed in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The university has substantial investments in fossil fuel companies, and in 2014 began consultations on whether it should follow some US universities which have committed to sell off their fossil fuel investments.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The university was one of the first in the UK to raise money through a major public fundraising campaign, the <a href="/wiki/Campaign_for_Oxford" title="Campaign for Oxford">Campaign for Oxford</a>. The current campaign, its second, was launched in May 2008 and is entitled "Oxford Thinking – The Campaign for the University of Oxford".<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> This is looking to support three areas: academic posts and programmes, student support, and buildings and infrastructure;<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> having passed its original target of £1.25&#160;billion in March 2012, the target was raised to £3&#160;billion.<sup id="cite_ref-financial-statements_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-financial-statements-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The campaign had raised a total of £2&#160;billion by May 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Affiliations">Affiliations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Oxford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Affiliations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Oxford is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Russell_Group" title="Russell Group">Russell Group</a> of research-led <a href="/wiki/Universities_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Universities in the United Kingdom">British universities</a>, the <a href="/wiki/G5_(education)" class="mw-redirect" title="G5 (education)">G5</a>, the <a href="/wiki/League_of_European_Research_Universities" title="League of European Research Universities">League of European Research Universities</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/International_Alliance_of_Research_Universities" title="International Alliance of Research Universities">International Alliance of Research Universities</a>. It is also a core member of the <a href="/wiki/Europaeum" title="Europaeum">Europaeum</a> and forms part of the "<a href="/wiki/Golden_triangle_(universities)" title="Golden triangle (universities)">golden triangle</a>" of highly research intensive and elite English universities.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<td><b><a href="/wiki/UCAS_Tariff" title="UCAS Tariff">Average Entry Tariff</a></b><sup id="cite_ref-Complete_League_Table_2019_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Complete_League_Table_2019-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup>
</td>
<td>n/a
</td>

</td>

</td>
